 7                   DIRECT EXAMINATION
 8    BY MS. DEAN:
 9        Q.  Good morning.  Can you hear me okay?
10        A.  Yes.
11        Q.  I promised you a beautiful view from
12    the 20th floor.
13            Did I follow through with my promises
14    so far?
15        A.  It's bit cloudy.
16        Q.  Dr. --

18    right?
19            THE WITNESS:  Yeah.
20            MS. DEAN:  I kept bragging about it.
21    BY MS. DEAN:
22        Q.  It says "Dr. Rigler" on here.
23            Are you a medical doctor?
24        A.  No.
0008
 1        Q.  Why do we use the name "doctor" with
 2    you?  What's the background?
 3        A.  That's -- the Ph.D. designation is a
 4    doctor, Doctor of Philosophy.
 5        Q.  Okay.  And what did you study?  What
 6    was your Ph.D. in?
 7        A.  It was in microbiology.
 8        Q.  I want to again just give the jury a
 9    context of why we asked you here.
10            Have you spent decades of your career
11    testing samples, human tissue and the air for
12    the presence of asbestos?
13        A.  Yes, I have.
14        Q.  In terms of backing up a little bit
15    about how you got there --
16        A.  All right.
17        Q.  -- I have your CV, which we have marked
18    as Exhibit 30.
19            MS. DEAN:  Your Honor, at this time
20    we'd like to admit that into evidence.
21            THE COURT:  Any objection?
22            MS. BUENO:  No objection.
23            THE COURT:  It will be allowed into
24    evidence.
0009
 1                     (Plaintiff's Exhibit 30 for ID
 2                      was admitted into Evidence.)
 3    BY MS. DEAN:
 4        Q.  What did you go to college for?  What
 5    did you get your undergraduate degree in?
 6        A.  My undergraduate degree was in biology.
 7        Q.  Even before you went to get your Ph.D.,
 8    did you have some experience using a
 9    transmission electron microscope?
10        A.  Yes.  And that was part of my training
11    at university.  I was research technician in the
12    agronomy department, which is plant sciences at
13    the University of Georgia, studying grasses and
14    the growth of grasses using the electron
15    microscope.
16        Q.  Is that something you used regularly
17    during that time?
18        A.  Yes.
19        Q.  Did you go on to get your Ph.D.?
20        A.  Yes.
21        Q.  Broadly speaking, can you tell us a
22    little bit about your doctoral training program
23    in the department of microbiology at the
24    University of Georgia?
0010
 1        A.  Sure.  In microbiology, we studied
 2    very, very small things, viruses, bacteria,
 3    particulates, all of those kinds of thing.  So
 4    you need the tools to be able to analyze and
 5    look at those.
 6            So in addition to things like advanced
 7    biochemistry, physics programs, nutrition, and
 8    electron microscopy, I focused in those areas to
 9    do the research, because that's part of your
10    research program.  And that's sort of it in a
11    nutshell.
12        Q.  Did that routinely have you involved in
13    both optical microscopy and electron microscopy?
14        A.  Yes.  I used those tools on a daily
15    basis.
16        Q.  Which one was this?
17        A.  Optical microscope.  Yes.
18        Q.  And this (indicating)?
19        A.  The transmission electron microscope.
20        Q.  Due to your work back when you were
21    getting your education and had nothing to do
22    with courtrooms, did you receive multiple awards
23    related specifically to your talents in
24    microscopy?
0011
 1        A.  Yes.  In the department I did research
 2    on pathogenic bacteria, that type of thing,
 3    learned electron microscopy, and I was awarded
 4    the Sigma Xi Award for my research work.
 5        Q.  And is that a scientific Honor Society?
 6        A.  Absolutely, yes.
 7        Q.  Did you receive other awards
 8    specifically related to your research work in
 9    the use of microscopy?
10        A.  Yes.  The Ruska Award that you see up
11    here was named for Ernst Ruska, who was one of
12    the early pioneers of electron microscopy, and
13    the Southeastern Electron Microscopy Society,
14    SEMS, also gave that award to students who were
15    doing research.  So I got that award also.
16        Q.  Did you also get the Presidential
17    Scholarship Award directly due to your work with
18    microscopy?
19        A.  Yes.  Yes.  And that's from the
20    Microscopy Society of America.
21        Q.  In terms of your professional career --
22    actually, I forgot one thing.
23            Were you also or at least for some time
24    teaching classes at Emery?
0012
 1        A.  I was.  I taught human anatomy at Emery
 2    University in Atlanta.
 3        Q.  Did you spend the vast majority of your
 4    career working at a specific lab?
 5        A.  Yes.  Materials Analytical Services in
 6    Norcross and then Suwanee, Georgia.
 7        Q.  If we see Material Analytical Services,
 8    is that the same thing as MAS?
 9        A.  Yes.  Yes.
10        Q.  When did you start there?
11        A.  I started there in 1989.
12        Q.  Even from the very beginning of your
13    long career at that lab, did you come in as just
14    an analyst or a more senior position?
15        A.  I came in at a bit more senior
16    position.
17        Q.  First, what is an analyst?  I kind of
18    jumped the gun there.
19        A.  That's someone who is actually doing
20    the analysis in the laboratory, preparing the
21    samples, analyzing the samples.  So that's what
22    your analyst is, technician.
23        Q.  Even though you weren't given the title
24    analyst or technician, were you routinely using
0013
 1    the various equipment at this lab, including the
 2    electron microscopes and the polarized light
 3    microscopes?
 4        A.  Yes.  When I was hired at the
 5    laboratory, that lab had three electron
 6    microscopes -- actually more than that.  They
 7    had four.  They had three TEMs, and they had an
 8    SEM and of course all the optical microscopes.
 9            So I learned a new electron microscopy
10    from the university, but I also applied that
11    there in the new setting.  I was the biologist
12    bringing biologicals also into that laboratory.
13    It was run by a material scientist.
14        Q.  Was there a point in your career where
15    the focus became less on doing the analytical
16    work and became more quality control,
17    supervision, and mentoring of the people doing
18    that work?
19        A.  Yes.  As I moved through my career at
20    MAS, I went to higher levels in -- as you can
21    see in my CV up there.
22        Q.  Just because we also have a written
23    record, you know, it notes that you moved from
24    vice president to different groups?
0014
 1        A.  Yes.
 2        Q.  One is the IH group.
 3            What does "IH" stand for?
 4        A.  That's industrial hygiene.  We had an
 5    industrial hygiene group.
 6        Q.  And the most basic level, what does an
 7    industrial hygienist do?
 8        A.  An industrial hygienist is mainly
 9    concerned with safety, worker safety, and what
10    they do is they measure the hazards that are in
11    the environment, and they quantify those
12    hazards.  And then they go to the company or the
13    client and explain what the hazards are, and
14    they try to keep them in compliance with the
15    laws.
16        Q.  Have you directly been involved with
17    that type of work at MAS?
18        A.  Yes.  I trained alongside numerous
19    industrial hygienists at MAS in the work that we
20    did because we had to do industrial hygiene
21    studies.  And so I worked with them and learned
22    those processes over probably three or four
23    years.
24        Q.  Specifically as it relates to asbestos
0015
 1    and detecting asbestos in that context, do you
 2    have education, training, and experience from an
 3    industrial hygiene perspective in evaluating
 4    asbestos in the air?
 5        A.  Yes.
 6        Q.  Did you go on to be a quality director,
 7    a senior consulting scientist, and then
 8    finally -- well, I don't know why I didn't
 9    underline the laboratory manager, but finally,
10    chief scientific officer?
11        A.  Yes.
12        Q.  Okay.  Have you contributed to the
13    peer-reviewed published literature?
14        A.  Yes, I have.
15        Q.  Are there dozens of articles that
16    you've written from a scientific perspective
17    that contributed to this literature?
18        A.  Yes.
19        Q.  Have some of them specifically related
20    to addressing and evaluating the presence of
21    asbestos in materials?
22        A.  Yes.  They have, yes.
23        Q.  Have multiple of those studies -- I
24    want to get even more specific -- related to
0016
 1    studying and analyzing concerns about asbestos
 2    in talc?
 3        A.  Yes.

 5        A.  Yes.

 7    reading the title so everyone knows what we're
 8    looking the?
 9        A.  "Cancer caused by exposure to asbestos
10    and fibrous talc in cosmetic talc powders, a
11    case series."
12        Q.  Was this a peer-reviewed published
13    paper?
14        A.  Yes, it was.

16    read every deposition taken in this case and try
17    to understand Theresa Garcia's full history,
18    right?
19        A.  That's correct, yes.

23        A.  Yes.  Yes.

 1    exposure history, based on what you reviewed?

 1        A.  No, we did not -- we didn't have that

 6        A.  Yes.

 9        A.  Yes.
10        Q.  Have you also been asked to be a member
11    or to help set standards for different
12    organizations?
13        A.  Yes, I have.  Yes.
14        Q.  We really haven't talked about
15    scientific and standard studying in the
16    organizations.
17            Is that a crucial part of what you do?
18        A.  Yes.  It's crucial to laboratories to
19    have that -- to be part of those kinds of

22    kind of thing.
23        Q.  What is ASTM?
24        A.  That's the American Society of Testing
0019
 1    and Materials.
 2        Q.  And in terms of specifically D22 Air
 3    Standard Committee, is that a committee where
 4    you are a member?
 5        A.  Yes.
 6        Q.  And does that involve asbestos
 7    concerns?
 8        A.  Yes.  Yes, it does.
 9        Q.  If you have someone who comes in and
10    tries to say:  Look, I'm looking at talc, I
11    don't find any asbestos, but they're using a
12    standard that is in any organizational standards
13    setting in the world, is that concerning to you
14    as a laboratory manager for years and as a
15    scientist?
16        A.  Yes, it is.  Yes.  They need to be
17    using the standards.
18        Q.  Are these two groups that you're
19    involved in?
20        A.  Yes.
21        Q.  Just first, what are they?
22        A.  AIHA is the American Industrial Hygiene
23    Association, and we just talked a little bit
24    about hygiene -- their group above industrial
0020
 1    hygienists and Ph.D.s and medical doctors who
 2    are reviewing and looking at worker safety
 3    protocols.
 4            The American College of Occupational
 5    and Environmental Medicine is mainly an
 6    organization of medical doctors and Ph.D.s who
 7    are looking also at industrial hygiene kinds of
 8    situations for worker safety.
 9        Q.  Do these organization require some
10    showing of proficiency or have some kind of
11    vetting before they allow a scientist --
12        A.  Yes.
13        Q.  -- to be in their --
14        A.  Yes.  The American College of
15    Occupational Environmental Medicine requires a
16    review by their board for application and
17    membership reception, and AIHA also looks at
18    your background and your experience in order to
19    be a member of the group.
20        Q.  Are you long members of both?
21        A.  Yes.
22        Q.  Are you currently members of both?
23        A.  I am.
24        Q.  Okay.  Very broadly, what's the
0021
 1    difference between a bulk analysis, an air
 2    sample and a tissue digestion?
 3        A.  A bulk analysis is where you're taking
 4    a set amount of material, whether it be a powder
 5    or solid material of some kind, be metal, rock,
 6    plastics, whatever, and then taking portions of
 7    that and then analyzing that.
 8            Hair samples are where, if I have an
 9    air pump and a filter cassette in this room, I
10    could take a sample of the air in this room and
11    catch it onto the filter, and then I would take
12    that filter and prepare it for microscopy to
13    analyze it and see how many particulates and
14    what kind we have.
15            For tissue digestion, if I was to take
16    a portion of tissue out of your body and then
17    digest it, prepare it, get rid of the organic
18    material and then have the refractory material,
19    we call, left over, then I could also put that
20    on the filter, analyze that with the electron
21    microscope or the optical microscope.
22        Q.  I'll represent to you that the jury was
23    told by the Johnson & Johnson chosen lawyer that
24    you're the tissue guy.
0022
 1        A.  Tissue guy.
 2        Q.  That we're bringing the tissue guy to
 3    talk about testing.
 4        A.  Okay.  Here I am.

 6    tissue guy, are you using a microscope like this
 7    one?
 8        A.  Yes.

10    testing guy?
11        A.  No.

15        A.  Yes.

21        A.  Yes.

23    many people that have said, I used cosmetic
24    talc, to see what was in their lungs?

 1        A.  Yes.

0024
 1    the ground to what we're finding in the bottle

 6        A.  Yes, it is.

0026
 1        Q.  And in terms of tissue digestion,

19        A.  Oh, yes, yes.
20        Q.  To back up to how the lab started
21    looking at cosmetic talc, we're going to talk
22    about the exact protocols that were used, but
23    before we get there, can you tell us as the
24    chief scientific officer, were you focused on
0027
 1    methods and method development?
 2        A.  Yes, I was.  Yes.
 3        Q.  What is a standard operating procedure?
 4        A.  That is a sort of like a checklist that
 5    pilots have in aircraft for the steps in the
 6    protocols that you're going through for the
 7    analysis procedure.
 8        Q.  Does it involve from preparation to
 9    what count to the actual tools that you use?  I
10    mean, is it kind of the cookbook for what you
11    do?
12        A.  Yes, all the way from the receiving of
13    a sample, reviewing the chain of custody,
14    looking at the condition of the samples when
15    they come in, photographing them, and then on up
16    through sample preparation, analysis, quality
17    control on the analysis, and then of course the
18    reporting.
19        Q.  In the time frame when you were working
20    to create the standard operating protocols for
21    testing for asbestos in talc --
22        A.  Yes.
23        Q.   -- did that have to finish before you
24    got into the lab with the analyst to do the
0028
 1    testing?
 2        A.  Yes.
 3        Q.  Before that, can you tell the jury a
 4    little bit about kind of a big project you were
 5    leading for the lab?
 6        A.  Yes.  We -- the laboratory wanted to
 7    become proficient in testing things like drugs
 8    and medical devices, that type of thing, so in
 9    order to get an FDA laboratory number, if you
10    will.
11            So that involves a pretty massive
12    quality control effort in your laboratory
13    whereby you review all of your protocols or
14    steps for whatever thing you're going to do.
15    Say it's a medical instrument or some kind of
16    medical device.  You've got to review all the
17    steps for whatever your analysis is, you've got
18    to put a protocol together, you've got to
19    demonstrate that the protocol works.  Over and
20    over and over again you've got to do quality
21    control on that, and this is a very long
22    process.  It can take years and in fact it did.
23        Q.  Who led that process for MAS?
24        A.  I did.
0029
 1        Q.  Did that involve FDA requirements for
 2    good laboratory practices and good manufacturing
 3    practices?
 4        A.  Yes.  That's somewhat of what I've
 5    described, GLPs and GMPs.  It looks simple, but
 6    it's extremely complicated and time consuming.
 7    It is a government mandated program.  They allow
 8    you to put your protocols together, but
 9    everything you do, you better do it and do it
10    according to the way you said you were going to
11    do it because they're going to check you on it.

 6    higher density, about 3.2.  So they're going to
 7    sink to the bottom when you put them in the
 8    density liquid.
 9            The things like talc and things like
10    chrysotile, they have a very similar density,
11    like 2.6, 2.7, something like that.  So they're
12    going to go up to the top.
13            So you're going to get the amphiboles,
14    the heavy ones, but the chrysotile, you're not
15    going to get those.  So that's some of the
16    balance.

13    say that's one.
14            And as you can see, there are many more
15    than one there.  So we may be undercounting
16    those structures.  But those are the rules.  We
17    have to follow the rules, the counting rules.
18        Q.  So whether you find a single fiber or a
19    bundle that has hundreds of fibers, that's
20    considered one structure?
21        A.  That is one structure, yes.
22        Q.  Sometimes we see nomenclature -- we're
23    almost to lunch, but if you need water --
24        A.  I'm good.  That might work perfectly.
0135
 1        Q.  Sometimes we see nomenclature of fibers
 2    and a cc?
 3        A.  Yes.
 4        Q.  Sometimes we see an S --
 5        A.  Yes.
 6        Q.  -- before cc?
 7            What does the S mean?
 8        A.  Structure.
 9        Q.  And all of those would either a single
10    fiber, a bundle, or a cluster of asbestos?
11        A.  Yes.
12        Q.  Is it important to make sure you're
13    differentiating platy talc from fibers and
14    bundles?
15        A.  Yes, because the vast majority of the
16    talc itself is composed of these plates, as you
17    can see on the left.  And then there are also
18    fibers there.  We also have talc fibers in the
19    mix.  And we need to be able to tell the
20    difference between the talc fibers and bundles
21    of the asbestos types in there.
22        Q.  Are the next two steps to make sure
23    that when you're showing pictures like this, we
24    are differentiating fibrous talc and asbestos?
0136
 1        A.  Yes.
 2        Q.  Is this example when you find fibers,
 3    bundles, matrices, or clusters of where you're
 4    supposed to as a scientist write that
 5    information down?
 6        A.  Right.  And this is what the
 7    laboratories do.  We have to document all of
 8    this.
 9            As you saw in the Cyprus report, their
10    bench sheet, same kinds of things.  We have to
11    go through there, and we have to find out the
12    length and the width.  We have to do the
13    chemistry and all of those things.  And we have
14    to record all of that and put it on our count
15    sheets.  That's part of the record.
16        Q.  And for every sample and every test
17    where you've shown and photograph asbestos in
18    baby powder, did you maintain all of these
19    records?
20        A.  Yes.
21        Q.  In terms of Step 2 and 3, I just want
22    orient the jury to that.
23            To try to make sure you were matching
24    up things right, are there standards of raw
0137
 1    asbestos that a lab can get to guide them?
 2        A.  Yes.
 3        Q.  Tell us a little bit about that.
 4        A.  Certain certifying agencies -- and this
 5    shows the NIST, the National Institute of
 6    Standards, they have the pure raw ore in
 7    asbestiform fibrous form.  As you can see,
 8    there's a vial of that over here on the right
 9    here.  And you can purchase those.  And that's
10    the actual standard that you make up and you put
11    on the electron microscope grid, and you put it
12    in and say, okay, I know what this is.  I know
13    what the chemistry is.  I know what the shape
14    is.  I know what the diffraction is.  So I know
15    what my standards are.
16        Q.  In terms of how that's used by you as a
17    scientist when you find a fiber, first, how do
18    you go from having a fiber to getting these
19    graphs, and then how do you use the standard to
20    make judgment calls about what you're looking
21    at?
22        A.  Okay.  So over here on the left is a
23    couple of tremolite asbestos fibers that are in
24    that standard we just talked about.
0138
 1            Now, the electron microscope is looking
 2    at this.  So you see this whole image because
 3    electrons are coming down through here and
 4    you're visualizing it.
 5            Now, there's a way that you can take
 6    that electron beam, as you see that little red
 7    spot, I can put it right there on that fiber.
 8    All right.  And then I'm going to get an X-ray
 9    signal back from that, which I come over here
10    and I accumulate counts for each one of these
11    mineral types.  This one right here is
12    magnesium.  This one is silicon.  This one over
13    here is iron -- excuse me, calcium.
14            So in tremolite asbestos, we have these
15    chemicals in it, and that's our profile there.
16    This is the same profile from this one.  If I
17    put the electron beam right on there, or
18    somewhere along here, I'm going to get this
19    profile.
20            So that's your -- what we call the EDS
21    or the energy dispersive spectroscopy.  And
22    that's a chemical fingerprint of it.
23        Q.  For everything?
24        A.  Yes.
0139
 1        Q.  To mix things up, do we have the same
 2    chemicals that we looked at, and then we look to
 3    see matches in terms of what chemicals you
 4    expect to see in the mineral and how much each?
 5        A.  Yes.
 6        Q.  So those peaks are important, not just
 7    where they are?
 8        A.  Correct.  And the proportions of the
 9    peaks are also important for that particular
10    kind of asbestos.  The ratio of those peaks is
11    pretty much set.
12            Now, there's -- you can have a little
13    bit of iron here.  As the iron comes up in this
14    specimen, we have what's called actinolite
15    because they're on a continuum to some degree in
16    the ground.  So I won't go into too much more of
17    that, but we want to look for these patterns.
18        Q.  Actually very quickly, what is a solid
19    solution series, and how does that relate to
20    evaluating chemistry?
21        A.  Okay.  Well, again, in the ground you
22    can have an input of iron from the surrounding
23    geologicals that are there.  And, for instance,
24    this peak over here, this iron peak can grow and
0140
 1    get much larger and then the magnesium peak
 2    comes down.
 3            And then you would have what's called
 4    actinolite.  And they are on a chemical spectrum
 5    in the ground because that's the way they grow
 6    in the ground.
 7            And so, you know, you can have those
 8    variations, and that's how we tell the
 9    differences between them.  It's a science
10    lesson.  You guys are going to become experts in
11    this.
12        Q.  So for every single fiber or bundle
13    that your lab photographed and found in baby
14    powder --
15        A.  Yes.
16        Q.  -- did you confirm which type of
17    asbestos you were looking at?
18        A.  Yes.
19        Q.  Did you find tremolite and
20    anthophyllite over and over again?
21        A.  Yes.
22        Q.  The next thing you mentioned, the
23    Step 3, were these diffraction patterns.
24        A.  Yes.
0141
 1        Q.  Which I called dots.
 2        A.  Yes.
 3        Q.  This is the one I struggle with the
 4    most.
 5        A.  Yes.
 6        Q.  Help us understand what diffraction
 7    patterns are and why they are important,
 8    confirming what you're looking at.
 9        A.  Okay.  So when you put the beam on it,
10    here, again, like you do with chemistry, I would
11    put the beam on that, and then from here what we
12    get is scattering of the electron beam when it
13    comes down here, because this is what -- the
14    electron beam is coming through here.
15            But if I narrow it down to a little
16    point like this, and it hits this crystal
17    because it's crystalline, it goes in here and it
18    bounces around like in a prism.  When you hold a
19    prism up to the light, the white light comes out
20    in rainbow patterns.
21            It's the same kind of thing going on
22    here, except this little dot, the electron beam,
23    goes in and hits the crystal sides and scatters
24    and it comes out and produces this pattern,
0142
 1    which is giving you the dimensions of the actual
 2    crystalline structure of that material.  And for
 3    tremolite it gives you these rows of spots.
 4            This is the actual electron beam being
 5    scattered and hitting the film down here in
 6    these patterns.  And it's very definitive for
 7    that material itself.  It's a fingerprint for
 8    it.
 9            Here's another one.  Same tremolite we
10    saw before, and this -- these row patterns are
11    very specific.  And what we do is we go in here
12    and we measure the distances between these rows.
13            And in the case of amphiboles, it's
14    about .53 nanometers, if you will.
15            So that's how much we can see, and it
16    gives us three ways to unequivocally identify
17    that that's what this is.
18        Q.  And so the pattern itself and how close
19    the different items in the pattern are to each
20    other are what allow someone with a scientific
21    background to say:  That's tremolite?
22        A.  Correct.

12    had to be lab contamination.  It had to be us,
13    even though all their blanks were clean.  So
14    this was in 2011, I believe it was.
15        Q.  In this scenario, was there an initial
16    report:  Our blanks and controls are clean.
17    This probably has nothing to do with lab
18    contamination.
19            Is that what they initially say?
20        A.  Yes.
21        Q.  After they tested the water, the floor
22    tiles, everything and couldn't even find caulk
23    on a window, did they just change their results
24    and blame it on the lab?
0293
 1        A.  Yes.
 2        Q.  As a person who's run the lab, is that
 3    appropriate or scientific?
 4        A.  No.  I mean, I understand the effort of
 5    trying to find what they believe was the source,
 6    but when your blanks are clean, I just don't
 7    understand.  It just seems like they do it
 8    backward.
 9            They do it backward from everywhere --
10    everybody else in the laboratory business.  You
11    look at the blanks first.  If there's an issue
12    with the blanks, you're going to have an issue
13    with your samples.  And you just stop.
14            And then you go, you look to see where
15    the problem possibly could be, and then you fix
16    it and you go back and do blanks again.  And if
17    they're clean, proceed with the analysis.
18            But they do it the other way around.
19    If they find something in the sample, we got
20    contamination.  So this is -- this is the way
21    they do it.
